# IMPORTANT: Source Code Access

**Local source files are not present.** Your workspace does not contain source code. You **MUST** use Sourcegraph MCP tools to discover, read, and understand code before making any changes.

**Target Repository:** `github.com/sg-benchmarks/postgres--5a461dc4`
- Use `repo:^github.com/sg-benchmarks/postgres--5a461dc4$` filter in keyword_search
- Use `github.com/sg-benchmarks/postgres--5a461dc4` as the `repo` parameter for go_to_definition/find_references/read_file


## Required Workflow

1. **Search first** — Use MCP tools to find relevant files and understand existing patterns
2. **Read remotely** — Use `sg_read_file` to read full file contents from Sourcegraph
3. **Edit locally** — Use Edit, Write, and Bash to create or modify files in your working directory
4. **Verify locally** — Run tests with Bash to check your changes

## Tool Selection

| Goal | Tool |
|------|------|
| Exact symbol/string | `sg_keyword_search` |
| Concepts/semantic search | `sg_nls_search` |
| Trace usage/callers | `sg_find_references` |
| See implementation | `sg_go_to_definition` |
| Read full file | `sg_read_file` |
| Browse structure | `sg_list_files` |
| Find repos | `sg_list_repos` |
| Search commits | `sg_commit_search` |
| Track changes | `sg_diff_search` |
| Compare versions | `sg_compare_revisions` |

**Decision logic:**
1. Know the exact symbol? → `sg_keyword_search`
2. Know the concept, not the name? → `sg_nls_search`
3. Need definition of a symbol? → `sg_go_to_definition`
4. Need all callers/references? → `sg_find_references`
5. Need full file content? → `sg_read_file`

## Scoping (Always Do This)

```
repo:^github.com/ORG/REPO$           # Exact repo (preferred)
repo:github.com/ORG/                 # All repos in org
file:.*\.ts$                         # TypeScript only
file:src/api/                        # Specific directory
```

Start narrow. Expand only if results are empty.

## Efficiency Rules

- Chain searches logically: search → read → references → definition
- Don't re-search for the same pattern; use results from prior calls
- Prefer `sg_keyword_search` over `sg_nls_search` when you have exact terms
- Read 2-3 related files before synthesising, rather than one at a time
- Don't read 20+ remote files without writing code — once you understand the pattern, start implementing

## If Stuck

If MCP search returns no results:
1. Broaden the search query (synonyms, partial identifiers)
2. Try `sg_nls_search` for semantic matching
3. Use `sg_list_files` to browse the directory structure
4. Use `sg_list_repos` to verify the repository name

# big-code-pg-arch-001: PostgreSQL Query Execution Pipeline

## Task

Trace the PostgreSQL query execution pipeline from parse to execute. Starting from `exec_simple_query()` in the traffic cop, map how a SQL string flows through the parser (lexer + grammar), semantic analyzer, query rewriter, planner/optimizer, and executor, identifying the data structures that flow between each stage.

## Context

- **Repository**: postgres/postgres (C, ~1.5M LOC)
- **Category**: Architectural Understanding
- **Difficulty**: hard
- **Subsystem Focus**: src/backend/ — parser, rewrite, optimizer, executor subsystems

## Requirements

1. Identify all files involved in the query pipeline (traffic cop, parser, analyzer, rewriter, planner, executor, node type definitions)
2. Trace the dependency chain from `exec_simple_query()` through each pipeline stage, identifying the data type transformations (RawStmt -> Query -> PlannedStmt -> tuples)
3. Document the two-phase optimization (Path generation via allpaths.c, then Plan creation via createplan.c)
4. Explain the Volcano-style executor dispatch in execProcnode.c

## Expected Output

Write your analysis to `/logs/agent/solution.md` with the following structure:

```
## Files Examined
- path/to/file1.ext — role in architecture
- path/to/file2.ext — role in architecture
...

## Dependency Chain
1. Entry point: path/to/entry.ext
2. Calls: path/to/next.ext (via function/method name)
3. Delegates to: path/to/impl.ext
...

## Analysis
[Detailed architectural analysis including:
- Design patterns identified
- Component responsibilities
- Data flow description
- Interface contracts between components]

## Summary
[Concise 2-3 sentence summary answering the task question]
```

## Evaluation Criteria

- File recall: Did you find the correct set of architecturally relevant files?
- Dependency accuracy: Did you trace the correct dependency/call chain?
- Architectural coherence: Did you correctly identify the design patterns and component relationships?

{"content":"1: src/backend/executor/README\n2: \n3: The Postgres Executor\n4: =====================\n5: \n6: The executor processes a tree of \"plan nodes\".  The plan tree is essentially\n7: a demand-pull pipeline of tuple processing operations.  Each node, when\n8: called, will produce the next tuple in its output sequence, or NULL if no\n9: more tuples are available.  If the node is not a primitive relation-scanning\n10: node, it will have child node(s) that it calls in turn to obtain input\n11: tuples.\n12: \n13: Refinements on this basic model include:\n14: \n15: * Choice of scan direction (forwards or backwards).  Caution: this is not\n16: currently well-supported.  It works for primitive scan nodes, but not very\n17: well for joins, aggregates, etc.\n18: \n19: * Rescan command to reset a node and make it generate its output sequence\n20: over again.\n21: \n22: * Parameters that can alter a node's results.  After adjusting a parameter,\n23: the rescan command must be applied to that node and all nodes above it.\n24: There is a moderately intelligent scheme to avoid rescanning nodes\n25: unnecessarily (for example, Sort does not rescan its input if no parameters\n26: of the input have changed, since it can just reread its stored sorted data).\n27: \n28: For a SELECT, it is only necessary to deliver the top-level result tuples\n29: to the client.  For INSERT/UPDATE/DELETE/MERGE, the actual table modification\n30: operations happen in a top-level ModifyTable plan node.  If the query\n31: includes a RETURNING clause, the ModifyTable node delivers the computed\n32: RETURNING rows as output, otherwise it returns nothing.  Handling INSERT\n33: is pretty straightforward: the tuples returned from the plan tree below\n34: ModifyTable are inserted into the correct result rel

Perfect! I've completed a comprehensive analysis of the PostgreSQL query execution pipeline and written it to `/logs/agent/solution.md`.

## Summary

The analysis traces a SQL query's journey through PostgreSQL's execution pipeline:

1. **Parsing** (parser.c, scan.l, gram.y) — Lexer + grammar convert SQL text to untyped RawStmt nodes
2. **Semantic Analysis** (analyze.c, parse_*.c) — Validates types, resolves names, produces typed Query nodes  
3. **Rewriting** (rewriteHandler.c) — Applies rules, expands views, injects security policies
4. **Planning - Phase 1** (allpaths.c) — Generates candidate execution Paths with cost estimates
5. **Planning - Phase 2** (createplan.c) — Converts optimal Path to executable Plan tree
6. **Execution - Init** (execProcnode.c) — Builds PlanState tree, compiles expressions, sets up function pointers
7. **Execution - Run** (execProcnode.c, nodeXXX.c) — **Volcano-style pull model**: each node calls `ExecProcNode()` on children to pull tuples, processes them, and returns results
8. **Execution - Cleanup** (execProcnode.c) — Recursively closes resources

The key architectural insights:
- **Volcano iterator model**: Tuple-at-a-time streaming via function pointer dispatch
- **Two-phase optimization**: Path generation (search space) then Plan creation (executable form)
- **Read-only plan trees**: Immutable plans enable caching; all state in PlanState tree
- **Expression compilation**: Expr trees converted to flat ExprEvalStep arrays for efficiency
- **Clean separation**: Each pipeline stage has clear responsibilities and interfaces

The solution is written to `/logs/agent/solution.md` with detailed file listings, dependency chains, design pattern analysis, and data flow documentation.

The plan tree is essentially\\n7: a demand-pull pipeline of tuple processing operations.  Each node, when\\n8: called, will produce the next tuple in its output sequence, or NULL if no\\n9: more tuples are available.  If the node is not a primitive relation-scanning\\n10: node, it will have child node(s) that it calls in turn to obtain input\\n11: tuples.\\n12: \\n13: Refinements on this basic model include:\\n14: \\n15: * Choice of scan direction (forwards or backwards).  Caution: this is not\\n16: currently well-supported.  It works for primitive scan nodes, but not very\\n17: well for joins, aggregates, etc.\\n18: \\n19: * Rescan command to reset a node and make it generate its output sequence\\n20: over again.\\n21: \\n22: * Parameters that can alter a node's results.  After adjusting a parameter,\\n23: the rescan command must be applied to that node and all nodes above it.\\n24: There is a moderately intelligent scheme to avoid rescanning nodes\\n25: unnecessarily (for example, Sort does not rescan its input if no parameters\\n26: of the input have changed, since it can just reread its stored sorted data).\\n27: \\n28: For a SELECT, it is only necessary to deliver the top-level result tuples\\n29: to the client.  For INSERT/UPDATE/DELETE/MERGE, the actual table modification\\n30: operations happen in a top-level ModifyTable plan node.  If the query\\n31: includes a RETURNING clause, the ModifyTable node delivers the computed\\n32: RETURNING rows as output, otherwise it returns nothing.  Handling INSERT\\n33: is pretty straightforward: the tuples returned from the plan tree below\\n34: ModifyTable are inserted into the correct result relation.  For UPDATE,\\n35: the plan tree returns the new values of the updated columns, plus \\\"junk\\\"\\n36: (hidden) column(s) identifying which table row is to be updated.  The\\n37: ModifyTable node must fetch that row to extract values for the unchanged\\n38: columns, combine the values into a new row, and apply the update.  (For a\\n39: heap table, the row-identity junk column is a CTID, but other things may\\n40: be used for other table types.)  For DELETE, the plan tree need only deliver\\n41: junk row-identity column(s), and the ModifyTable node visits each of those\\n42: rows and marks the row deleted.  MERGE is described below.\\n43: \\n44: XXX a great deal more documentation needs to be written here...\\n45: \\n46: \\n47: Plan Trees and State Trees\\n48: --------------------------\\n49: \\n50: The plan tree delivered by the planner contains a tree of Plan nodes (struct\\n51: types derived from struct Plan).  During executor startup we build a parallel\\n52: tree of identical structure containing executor state nodes --- generally,\\n53: every plan node type has a corresponding executor state node type.  Each node\\n54: in the state tree has a pointer to its corresponding node in the plan tree,\\n55: plus executor state data as needed to implement that node type.  This\\n56: arrangement allows the plan tree to be completely read-only so far as the\\n57: executor is concerned: all data that is modified during execution is in the\\n58: state tree.  Read-only plan trees make life much simpler for plan caching and\\n59: reuse.\\n60: \\n61: A corresponding executor state node may not be created during executor startup\\n62: if the executor determines that an entire subplan is not required due to\\n63: execution time partition pruning determining that no matching records will be\\n64: found there.  This currently only occurs for Append and MergeAppend nodes.  In\\n65: this case the non-required subplans are ignored and the executor state's\\n66: subnode array will become out of sequence to the plan's subplan list.\\n67: \\n68: Each Plan node may have expression trees associated with it, to represent\\n69: its target list, qualification conditions, etc.  These trees are also\\n70: read-only to the executor, but the executor state for expression evaluation\\n71: does not mirror the Plan expression's tree shape, as explained below.\\n72: Rather, there's just one ExprState node per expression tree, although this\\n73: may have sub-nodes for some complex expression node types.\\n74: \\n75: Altogether there are four classes of nodes used in these trees: Plan nodes,\\n76: their corresponding PlanState nodes, Expr nodes, and ExprState nodes.\\n77: (Actually, there are also List nodes, which are used as \\\"glue\\\" in all\\n78: three tree-based representations.)\\n79: \\n80: \\n81: Expression Trees and ExprState nodes\\n82: ------------------------------------\\n83: \\n84: Expression trees, in contrast to Plan trees, are not mirrored into a\\n85: corresponding tree of state nodes.  Instead each separately executable\\n86: expression tree (e.g. a Plan's qual or targetlist) is represented by one\\n87: ExprState node.  The ExprState node contains the information needed to\\n88: evaluate the expression in a compact, linear form.  That compact form is\\n89: stored as a flat array in ExprState->steps[] (an array of ExprEvalStep,\\n90: not ExprEvalStep *).\\n91: \\n92: The reasons for choosing such a representation include:\\n93: - commonly the amount of work needed to evaluate one Expr-type node is\\n94:   small enough that the overhead of having to perform a tree-walk\\n95:   during evaluation is significant.\\n96: - the flat representation can be evaluated non-recursively within a single\\n97:   function, reducing stack depth and function call overhead.\\n98: - such a representation is usable both for fast interpreted execution,\\n99:   and for compiling into native code.\\n100: \\n101: The Plan-tree representation of an expression is compiled into an\\n102: ExprState node by ExecInitExpr().  As much complexity as possible should\\n103: be handled by ExecInitExpr() (and helpers), instead of execution time\\n104: where both interpreted and compiled versions would need to deal with the\\n105: complexity.  Besides duplicating effort between execution approaches,\\n106: runtime initialization checks also have a small but noticeable cost every\\n107: time the expression is evaluated.  Therefore, we allow ExecInitExpr() to\\n108: precompute information that we do not expect to vary across execution of a\\n109: single query, for example the set of CHECK constraint expressions to be\\n110: applied to a domain type.  This could not be done at plan time without\\n111: greatly increasing the number of events that require plan invalidation.\\n112: (Previously, some information of this kind was rechecked on each\\n113: expression evaluation, but that seems like unnecessary overhead.)\\n114: \\n115: \\n116: Expression Initialization\\n117: -------------------------\\n118: \\n119: During ExecInitExpr() and similar routines, Expr trees are converted\\n120: into the flat representation.  Each Expr node might be represented by\\n121: zero, one, or more ExprEvalSteps.\\n122: \\n123: Each ExprEvalStep's work is determined by its opcode (of enum ExprEvalOp)\\n124: and it stores the result of its work into the Datum variable and boolean\\n125: null flag variable pointed to by ExprEvalStep->resvalue/resnull.\\n126: Complex expressions are performed by chaining together several steps.\\n127: For example, \\\"a + b\\\" (one OpExpr, with two Var expressions) would be\\n128: represented as two steps to fetch the Var values, and one step for the\\n129: evaluation of the function underlying the + operator.  The steps for the\\n130: Vars would have their resvalue/resnull pointing directly to the appropriate\\n131: args[].value .isnull elements in the FunctionCallInfoBaseData struct that\\n132: is used by the function evaluation step, thus avoiding extra work to copy\\n133: the result values around.\\n134: \\n135: The last entry in a completed ExprState->steps array is always an\\n136: EEOP_DONE_RETURN or EEOP_DONE_NO_RETURN step; this removes the need to\\n137: test for end-of-array while iterating. The former is used when the\\n138: expression returns a value directly, the latter when side-effects of\\n139: expression initialization are the goal (e.g. for projection or\\n140: aggregate transition value computation).\\n141: \\n142: Also, if the expression contains any variable references (to user\\n143: columns of the ExprContext's INNER, OUTER, or SCAN tuples), the steps\\n144: array begins with EEOP_*_FETCHSOME steps that ensure that the relevant\\n145: tuples have been deconstructed to make the required columns directly\\n146: available (cf. slot_getsomeattrs()).  This allows individual Var-fetching\\n147: steps to be little more than an array lookup.\\n148: \\n149: Most of ExecInitExpr()'s work is done by the recursive function\\n150: ExecInitExprRec() and its subroutines. For INSERT/UPDATE/DELETE/MERGE, the actual table modification\n30: operations happen in a top-level ModifyTable plan node.  If the query\n31: includes a RETURNING clause, the ModifyTable node delivers the computed\n32: RETURNING rows as output, otherwise it returns nothing.  Handling INSERT\n33: is pretty straightforward: the tuples returned from the plan tree below\n34: ModifyTable are inserted into the correct result relation.  For UPDATE,\n35: the plan tree returns the new values of the updated columns, plus \"junk\"\n36: (hidden) column(s) identifying which table row is to be updated.  The\n37: ModifyTable node must fetch that row to extract values for the unchanged\n38: columns, combine the values into a new row, and apply the update.  (For a\n39: heap table, the row-identity junk column is a CTID, but other things may\n40: be used for other table types.)  For DELETE, the plan tree need only deliver\n41: junk row-identity column(s), and the ModifyTable node visits each of those\n42: rows and marks the row deleted.  MERGE is described below.\n43: \n44: XXX a great deal more documentation needs to be written here...\n45: \n46: \n47: Plan Trees and State Trees\n48: --------------------------\n49: \n50: The plan tree delivered by the planner contains a tree of Plan nodes (struct\n51: types derived from struct Plan).  During executor startup we build a parallel\n52: tree of identical structure containing executor state nodes --- generally,\n53: every plan node type has a corresponding executor state node type.  Each node\n54: in the state tree has a pointer to its corresponding node in the plan tree,\n55: plus executor state data as needed to implement that node type.  This\n56: arrangement allows the plan tree to be completely read-only so far as the\n57: executor is concerned: all data that is modified during execution is in the\n58: state tree.  Read-only plan trees make life much simpler for plan caching and\n59: reuse.\n60: \n61: A corresponding executor state node may not be created during executor startup\n62: if the executor determines that an entire subplan is not required due to\n63: execution time partition pruning determining that no matching records will be\n64: found there.  This currently only occurs for Append and MergeAppend nodes.  In\n65: this case the non-required subplans are ignored and the executor state's\n66: subnode array will become out of sequence to the plan's subplan list.\n67: \n68: Each Plan node may have expression trees associated with it, to represent\n69: its target list, qualification conditions, etc.  These trees are also\n70: read-only to the executor, but the executor state for expression evaluation\n71: does not mirror the Plan expression's tree shape, as explained below.\n72: Rather, there's just one ExprState node per expression tree, although this\n73: may have sub-nodes for some complex expression node types.\n74: \n75: Altogether there are four classes of nodes used in these trees: Plan nodes,\n76: their corresponding PlanState nodes, Expr nodes, and ExprState nodes.\n77: (Actually, there are also List nodes, which are used as \"glue\" in all\n78: three tree-based representations.)\n79: \n80: \n81: Expression Trees and ExprState nodes\n82: ------------------------------------\n83: \n84: Expression trees, in contrast to Plan trees, are not mirrored into a\n85: corresponding tree of state nodes.  Instead each separately executable\n86: expression tree (e.g. a Plan's qual or targetlist) is represented by one\n87: ExprState node.  The ExprState node contains the information needed to\n88: evaluate the expression in a compact, linear form.  That compact form is\n89: stored as a flat array in ExprState->steps[] (an array of ExprEvalStep,\n90: not ExprEvalStep *).\n91: \n92: The reasons for choosing such a representation include:\n93: - commonly the amount of work needed to evaluate one Expr-type node is\n94:   small enough that the overhead of having to perform a tree-walk\n95:   during evaluation is significant.\n96: - the flat representation can be evaluated non-recursively within a single\n97:   function, reducing stack depth and function call overhead.\n98: - such a representation is usable both for fast interpreted execution,\n99:   and for compiling into native code.\n100: \n101: The Plan-tree representation of an expression is compiled into an\n102: ExprState node by ExecInitExpr().  As much complexity as possible should\n103: be handled by ExecInitExpr() (and helpers), instead of execution time\n104: where both interpreted and compiled versions would need to deal with the\n105: complexity.  Besides duplicating effort between execution approaches,\n106: runtime initialization checks also have a small but noticeable cost every\n107: time the expression is evaluated.  Therefore, we allow ExecInitExpr() to\n108: precompute information that we do not expect to vary across execution of a\n109: single query, for example the set of CHECK constraint expressions to be\n110: applied to a domain type.  This could not be done at plan time without\n111: greatly increasing the number of events that require plan invalidation.\n112: (Previously, some information of this kind was rechecked on each\n113: expression evaluation, but that seems like unnecessary overhead.)\n114: \n115: \n116: Expression Initialization\n117: -------------------------\n118: \n119: During ExecInitExpr() and similar routines, Expr trees are converted\n120: into the flat representation.  Each Expr node might be represented by\n121: zero, one, or more ExprEvalSteps.\n122: \n123: Each ExprEvalStep's work is determined by its opcode (of enum ExprEvalOp)\n124: and it stores the result of its work into the Datum variable and boolean\n125: null flag variable pointed to by ExprEvalStep->resvalue/resnull.\n126: Complex expressions are performed by chaining together several steps.\n127: For example, \"a + b\" (one OpExpr, with two Var expressions) would be\n128: represented as two steps to fetch the Var values, and one step for the\n129: evaluation of the function underlying the + operator.  The steps for the\n130: Vars would have their resvalue/resnull pointing directly to the appropriate\n131: args[].value .isnull elements in the FunctionCallInfoBaseData struct that\n132: is used by the function evaluation step, thus avoiding extra work to copy\n133: the result values around.\n134: \n135: The last entry in a completed ExprState->steps array is always an\n136: EEOP_DONE_RETURN or EEOP_DONE_NO_RETURN step; this removes the need to\n137: test for end-of-array while iterating. The former is used when the\n138: expression returns a value directly, the latter when side-effects of\n139: expression initialization are the goal (e.g. for projection or\n140: aggregate transition value computation).\n141: \n142: Also, if the expression contains any variable references (to user\n143: columns of the ExprContext's INNER, OUTER, or SCAN tuples), the steps\n144: array begins with EEOP_*_FETCHSOME steps that ensure that the relevant\n145: tuples have been deconstructed to make the required columns directly\n146: available (cf. slot_getsomeattrs()).  This allows individual Var-fetching\n147: steps to be little more than an array lookup.\n148: \n149: Most of ExecInitExpr()'s work is done by the recursive function\n150: ExecInitExprRec() and its subroutines.  ExecInitExprRec() maps one Expr\n151: ",
    "url": "https://sourcegraph.sourcegraph.com/github.com/sg-benchmarks/postgres--5a461dc4@eb8f07549ee15f0db57589c5f333de3f874fe855/-/blob/src/backend/executor/README%3FL1-150"

REWRITING STAGE\n    \u2193\npg_rewrite_query() [src/backend/tcop/postgres.c:798]\n    \u2193\nQueryRewrite() [src/backend/rewrite/rewriteHandler.c]\n    \u2193\nApplies rules, view expansion, security policies\nReturns: List of Query nodes (0..n)\n\n    \u2193\n4.  \u2193\nstandard_ExecutorRun() [src/backend/executor/execMain.c:307]\n    \u251c\u2500 Startup dest receiver\n    \u2514\u2500 ExecutePlan() [src/backend/executor/execMain.c]\n        \u2514\u2500 node's ExecXxx() function\n            \u2502   \u251c\u2500 Calls ExecProcNode() on children to pull input tuples\n            \u2502   \u251c\u2500 Processes (filter, project, join, aggregate, etc.)\n            \u2502   \u2514\u2500 Returns output TupleTableSlot or NULL\n            \u2514\u2500 Repeats until ExecProcNode returns NULL (EOF)\n    \u2193\nExecutorFinish() [src/backend/executor/execMain.c]\n    \u2514\u2500 ExecFinishNode() - finalize aggregates, etc.\n    \u2193\nExecutorEnd() [src/backend/executor/execMain.c]\n    \u2514\u2500 ExecEndNode() - cleanup via switch dispatch\n```\n\n---\n\n## Analysis\n\n### Design Patterns Identified\n\n#### 1. **Volcano-Style Execution Model (Iterator Model)**\nPostgreSQL implements the classic Volcano/Cascades iterator execution model:\n- Each plan node acts as an iterator with three main operations:\n  - **Init** (`ExecInitNode`, dispatches to `ExecInitXxx`): Initialize state\n  - **Next** (`ExecProcNode`, dispatches to node-specific executor function): Pull one tuple\n  - **End** (`ExecEndNode`, dispatches to `ExecEndXxx`): Clean up\n\n- **Pull-based pipeline**: Execution flows bottom-up; parent nodes pull tuples from children via `ExecProcNode()` calls\n- **Tuple-at-a-time processing**: Each `ExecProcNode()` call returns exactly one tuple (or NULL for EOF)\n- **Function pointer dispatch**: During init, each PlanState node gets a function pointer (`ExecProcNode`) pointing to its executor function\n- **Recursive composition**: Complex queries are built from simple node types; nodes at all levels follow the same iterator interface\n\nThe inline `ExecProcNode()` (executor.h:310) is the performance-critical dispatch mechanism:\n```c\nstatic inline TupleTableSlot *\nExecProcNode(PlanState *node) {\n    if (node->chgParam != NULL)\n        ExecReScan(node);\n    return node->ExecProcNode(node);  // Function pointer dispatch\n}\n```\n\nThis design enables:\n- **Modularity**: New node types added by implementing Init/Next/End functions\n- **Composability**: Complex execution plans from simple, reusable components\n- **Memory efficiency**: Streaming tuple processing (no materializing entire result sets)\n\n#### 2. **Two-Phase Optimization**\nThe planner decomposes optimization into distinct phases:\n\n**Phase 1 \u2013 Path Generation (allpaths.c)**\n- Generates candidate execution paths for each relation and join combination\n- Each Path represents one possible way to access/process data\n- Paths include cost information (startup + total cost estimates)\n- Examples: SeqScan path, IndexScan path, BitmapIndexScan path\n- Joins generate: NestLoopPath, MergeJoinPath, HashJoinPath\n- Products: RelOptInfo with list of Paths; winner selected by cost\n\n**Phase 2 \u2013 Plan Creation (createplan.c)**\n- Converts optimal Path to executable Plan tree\n- `create_plan()` recursively traverses Path tree\n- Populates Plan nodes with node-specific initialization data\n- Example: HashJoinPath \u2192 HashJoin plan with hash table building strategy\n- Result: Plan tree ready for execution (schema-aware, all properties resolved)\n\nThis two-phase separation provides:\n- **Search space exploration** without committing to execution representation\n- **Cost-based decisions** with flexible path representations\n- **Clean interface** between optimization and execution (Path vs Plan)\n\n#### 3. **Read-Only Plan Trees**\n- Plan tree (src/include/nodes/plannodes.h) is completely immutable during execution\n- All mutable state is in the parallel PlanState tree (execnodes.h)\n- Enables safe plan caching and reuse across multiple executions\n- Separation of concerns: Planner produces schema; Executor manages state\n\n#### 4. **Expression Compilation to Flat Form**\n- Expr trees (parse tree representation) are compiled to flat ExprEvalStep arrays during ExecutorStart\n- `ExecInitExpr()` builds ExprState with steps[] array\n- Benefits:\n  - Reduces tree-walk overhead during evaluation\n  - Single-function interpretation of expressions\n  - Enables JIT compilation without interpreter overhead\n  - Precomputes invariant information\n\n#### 5. **State Tree Mirrors Plan Tree Structure**\n- During ExecutorStart, a PlanState tree is built with identical structure to Plan tree\n- Each Plan node \u2192 corresponding PlanState node type\n- Example: `Plan::Append` \u2192 `PlanState::AppendState`\n- PlanState holds all mutable data needed during execution\n- Allows plan reuse across multiple executions\n\n### Component Responsibilities\n\n**Traffic Cop (postgres.c:1011)**\n- Receives raw SQL string\n- Coordinates entire pipeline\n- Manages transaction context\n- Handles command completion and result delivery\n\n**Parser (parser.c + scan.l + gram.y)**\n- **Responsibilities**: Lexical + syntactic analysis\n- **Input**: SQL text string\n- **Output**: Parse tree (RawStmt with generic structure)\n- **Mechanism**: Flex lexer (scan.l) tokenizes; Bison parser (gram.y) builds tree\n- **Key Property**: No semantic validation; purely structural\n\n**Semantic Analyzer (analyze.c + parse_*.c)**\n- **Responsibilities**: Semantic validation, type checking, name resolution\n- **Input**: RawStmt (untyped parse tree)\n- **Output**: Query (semantically valid, type-checked)\n- **Process**:\n  - Validates table/column names against schema\n  - Type-checks expressions and coerces as needed\n  - Resolves ambiguous names\n  - Processes special clauses (aggregates, window functions, CTEs)\n- **Key Property**: Single Query per input RawStmt (usually)\n\n**Query Rewriter (rewriteHandler.c)**\n- **Responsibilities**: Rule application, view expansion, security policies\n- **Input**: Query (semantic tree)\n- **Output**: List of Queries (0..n due to expansion)\n- **Examples**:\n  - View \u2192 subquery in FROM\n  - Rule \u2192 additional auxiliary queries\n  - RLS policies \u2192 WHERE clause additions\n- **Key Property**: May expand 1 Query into many (SELECT + INSERT rule \u2192 2 queries)\n\n**Planner - Phase 1 (allpaths.c)**\n- **Responsibilities**: Generate candidate execution paths\n- **Input**: Query (semantic tree)\n- **Scope**: Base relations, joins, aggregations, etc.\n- **Process**:\n  - `generate_base_rel_paths()`: Paths for single tables\n  - `add_paths_to_joinrel()`: Join paths via nested loop, merge, hash\n  - Cost estimation via `cost_*()` functions\n- **Output**: RelOptInfo with alternative Paths (no execution specifics yet)\n\n**Planner - Phase 2 (createplan.c)**\n- **Responsibilities**: Convert optimal Path to Plan\n- **Input**: Best Path from Phase 1\n- **Process**: Recursive `create_plan()` dispatch\n  - Path type \u2192 Plan type\n  - Populates Plan node with execution parameters\n  - Example: HashPath \u2192 HashJoin with hash function, key columns\n- **Output**: Plan tree (execution-ready, immutable)\n\n**Executor - Init (execMain.c + execProcnode.c)**\n- **Responsibilities**: Build execution state tree\n- **Input**: Plan tree\n- **Process**: ExecInitNode recursive dispatch\n  - Each Plan node \u2192 PlanState node\n  - Compile expressions to ExprState\n  - Allocate runtime data structures\n  - Set up function pointers (ExecProcNode)\n- **Output**: PlanState tree with initialized state\n\n**Executor - Run (execMain.c + execProcnode.c + nodeXXX.c)**\n- **Responsibilities**: Pull tuples through pipeline\n- **Mechanism**: ExecProcNode inline dispatch\n  - Calls node function pointers\n  - Each node pulls from children\n  - Processes (filter, project, join, aggregate)\n  - Returns tuples or NULL (EOF)\n- **Key Property**: Tuple-at-a-time; streaming; no intermediate materialization (unless Sort, Agg, etc.)\n\n**Executor - End (execProcnode.c)**\n- **Responsibilities**: Clean up resources\n- **Process**: ExecEndNode recursive dispatch\n- **Handles**: Closing scans, freeing buffers, finalizing aggregates\n\n### Data Flow: Type Transformations\n\n```\nSQL String\n    \u2193\n    \u2514\u2192 raw_parser() [scan.l + gram.y]\n        \u2193\n        \u2514\u2192 RawStmt (untyped generic structure)\n            - Minimal semantic info\n            - No type checking\n            - No name resolution\n            \u2193\n            \u2514\u2192 parse_analyze() [analyze.c + parse_*.c]\n                \u2193\n                \u2514\u2192 Query (type-checked, name-resolved)\n                    - Full semantic information\n                    - Expression types resolved\n                    - Table/column references validated\n                    \u2193\n                    \u2514\u2192 QueryRewrite() [rewriteHandler.c]\n                        \u2193\n                        \u2514\u2192 Query (possibly expanded)\n                            - Rules applied\n                            - Views expanded\n                            - Security policies injected\n                            \u2193\n                            \u2514\u2192 standard_planner() [planner.c]\n                                \u251c\u2500 Phase 1: allpaths.c\n                                \u2502   \u2514\u2192 RelOptInfo (Paths with cost)\n                                \u2514\u2500 Phase 2: createplan.c\n                                    \u2193\n                                    \u2514\u2192 PlannedStmt (Plan tree + metadata)\n                                        - Executable plan\n                                        - All joins, aggregates planned\n                                        - Immutable during execution\n                                        \u2193\n                                        \u2514\u2192 ExecutorStart() [execMain.c]\n                                            \u2193\n                                            \u2514\u2192 ExecInitNode() [execProcnode.c]\n                                                \u2193\n                           - Function pointers set\n                                                    \u2193\n                                                    \u2514\u2192 ExecutorRun()\n                                                        \u2514\u2192 ExecProcNode() loop\n                                                            \u2193\n                                                            \u2514\u2192 Tuples (result set)\n```\n\n### Interface Contracts Between Components\n\n**Parser \u2192 Semantic Analyzer**\n- **Contract**: RawStmt with untyped expressions\n- **Guarantees**: Syntactically valid; may contain name/type errors\n- **Properties**: Generic structure (no knowledge of command type)\n\n**Semantic Analyzer \u2192 Rewriter**\n- **Contract**: Query with resolved types and names\n- **Guarantees**: Semantically valid within current schema; expressions typed\n- **Properties**: One-to-one mapping (usually) with input\n\n**Rewriter \u2192 Planner**\n- **Contract**: Query (may be from rewriter expansion)\n- **Guarantees**: Fully analyzed; ready for optimization\n- **Properties**: Multiple queries possible (parallel independent optimization)\n\n**Planner \u2192 Executor**\n- **Contract**: PlannedStmt with Plan tree\n- **Guarantees**: Optimal execution path selected; all joins/aggregates planned\n- **Properties**: Immutable; can be cached and reused\n\n**Executor Init \u2192 Executor Run**\n- **Contract**: PlanState tree with initialized state\n- **Guarantees**: All runtime structures allocated; expressions compiled\n- **Properties**: State tree mirrors plan structure; function pointers active\n\n### Volcano-Style Executor Dispatch (execProcnode.c)\n\nThe core dispatch mechanism at execution time is the **pull model**:\n\n**ExecProcNode inline dispatch (executor.h:310)**:\n```c\nstatic inline TupleTableSlot *\nExecProcNode(PlanState *node) {\n    if (node->chgParam != NULL)\n        ExecReScan(node);\n    return node->ExecProcNode(node);  // Function pointer\n}\n```\n\n**Dispatch during init** (`ExecInitNode`, execProcnode.c:142):\n```\nswitch (nodeTag(plan)) {\n    case T_SeqScan:\n        result = (PlanState *)ExecInitSeqScan((SeqScan *)node, ...);\n        // This sets: result->ExecProcNode = ExecSeqScan\n        break;\n    case T_NestLoop:\n        result = (PlanState *)ExecInitNestLoop((NestLoop *)node, ...);\n        // This sets: result->ExecProcNode = ExecNestLoop\n        break;\n    ...\n}\n```\n\n**Execution via function pointers** (nodeXXX.c):\n- `ExecSeqScan()` [nodeSeqscan.c]: Fetches tuples from heap sequentially\n- `ExecNestLoop()` [nodeNestloop.c]: Pulls from outer, for each outer pulls from inner, returns joined tuples\n- `ExecAgg()` [nodeAgg.c]: Accumulates tuples, returns aggregated results\n- `ExecSort()` [nodeSort.c]: Collects all input, sorts, returns in order\n- Each function:\n  1. Calls `ExecProcNode()` on children to get input tuples\n  2. Processes the tuple (filtering, projection, joining, aggregating)\n  3. Returns result tuple or NULL (EOF)\n\n**Pipeline execution in ExecutePlan()** (execMain.c:~350):\n```\nwhile (true) {\n    slot = ExecProcNode(topNode);  // Pull from root\n    if (TupIsNull(slot)) break;     // EOF\n    dest->receiveSlot(slot);         // Send to client\n}\n```\n\nThis creates a **demand-driven pipeline**:\n- Tuples flow bottom-up (pulled by parents)\n- No global queue/buffer (except for Sort, Agg, etc.)\n- Natural backpressure (if parent slow, child waits)\n- Scales to large result sets (streaming)\n\n---\n\n## Summary\n\nPostgreSQL implements a **classic multi-stage query execution pipeline** with clear separation of concerns. The **parser** (lexer + grammar) produces untyped parse trees; the **semantic analyzer** validates and types them; the **rewriter** applies rules and view expansion; the **planner** executes two-phase optimization (path generation then plan creation) to find the optimal execution strategy; and the **Volcano-style executor** pulls tuples through a tree of iterator nodes, with each node dispatched via function pointers to its type-specific implementation. This architecture achieves modularity, composability, and efficiency through clean interfaces between pipeline stages and a demand-driven execution model that streams results without materializing intermediate datasets.\n",

via switch dispatch\n```\n\n---\n\n## Analysis\n\n### Design Patterns Identified\n\n#### 1. **Volcano-Style Execution Model (Iterator Model)**\nPostgreSQL implements the classic Volcano/Cascades iterator execution model:\n- Each plan node acts as an iterator with three main operations:\n  - **Init** (`ExecInitNode`, dispatches to `ExecInitXxx`): Initialize state\n  - **Next** (`ExecProcNode`, dispatches to node-specific executor function): Pull one tuple\n  - **End** (`ExecEndNode`, dispatches to `ExecEndXxx`): Clean up\n\n- **Pull-based pipeline**: Execution flows bottom-up; parent nodes pull tuples from children via `ExecProcNode()` calls\n- **Tuple-at-a-time processing**: Each `ExecProcNode()` call returns exactly one tuple (or NULL for EOF)\n- **Function pointer dispatch**: During init, each PlanState node gets a function pointer (`ExecProcNode`) pointing to its executor function\n- **Recursive composition**: Complex queries are built from simple node types; nodes at all levels follow the same iterator interface\n\nThe inline `ExecProcNode()` (executor.h:310) is the performance-critical dispatch mechanism:\n```c\nstatic inline TupleTableSlot *\nExecProcNode(PlanState *node) {\n    if (node->chgParam != NULL)\n        ExecReScan(node);\n    return node->ExecProcNode(node);  // Function pointer dispatch\n}\n```\n\nThis design enables:\n- **Modularity**: New node types added by implementing Init/Next/End functions\n- **Composability**: Complex execution plans from simple, reusable components\n- **Memory efficiency**: Streaming tuple processing (no materializing entire result sets)\n\n#### 2. **Two-Phase Optimization**\nThe planner decomposes optimization into distinct phases:\n\n**Phase 1 \u2013 Path Generation (allpaths.c)**\n- Generates candidate execution paths for each relation and join combination\n- Each Path represents one possible way to access/process data\n- Paths include cost information (startup + total cost estimates)\n- Examples: SeqScan path, IndexScan path, BitmapIndexScan path\n- Joins generate: NestLoopPath, MergeJoinPath, HashJoinPath\n- Products: RelOptInfo with list of Paths; winner selected by cost\n\n**Phase 2 \u2013 Plan Creation (createplan.c)**\n- Converts optimal Path to executable Plan tree\n- `create_plan()` recursively traverses Path tree\n- Populates Plan nodes with node-specific initialization data\n- Example: HashJoinPath \u2192 HashJoin plan with hash table building strategy\n- Result: Plan tree ready for execution (schema-aware, all properties resolved)\n\nThis two-phase separation provides:\n- **Search space exploration** without committing to execution representation\n- **Cost-based decisions** with flexible path representations\n- **Clean interface** between optimization and execution (Path vs Plan)\n\n#### 3. **Read-Only Plan Trees**\n- Plan tree (src/include/nodes/plannodes.h) is completely immutable during execution\n- All mutable state is in the parallel PlanState tree (execnodes.h)\n- Enables safe plan caching and reuse across multiple executions\n- Separation of concerns: Planner produces schema; Executor manages state\n\n#### 4. **Expression Compilation to Flat Form**\n- Expr trees (parse tree representation) are compiled to flat ExprEvalStep arrays during ExecutorStart\n- `ExecInitExpr()` builds ExprState with steps[] array\n- Benefits:\n  - Reduces tree-walk overhead during evaluation\n  - Single-function interpretation of expressions\n  - Enables JIT compilation without interpreter overhead\n  - Precomputes invariant information\n\n#### 5. **State Tree Mirrors Plan Tree Structure**\n- During ExecutorStart, a PlanState tree is built with identical structure to Plan tree\n- Each Plan node \u2192 corresponding PlanState node type\n- Example: `Plan::Append` \u2192 `PlanState::AppendState`\n- PlanState holds all mutable data needed during execution\n- Allows plan reuse across multiple executions\n\n### Component Responsibilities\n\n**Traffic Cop (postgres.c:1011)**\n- Receives raw SQL string\n- Coordinates entire pipeline\n- Manages transaction context\n- Handles command completion and result delivery\n\n**Parser (parser.c + scan.l + gram.y)**\n- **Responsibilities**: Lexical + syntactic analysis\n- **Input**: SQL text string\n- **Output**: Parse tree (RawStmt with generic structure)\n- **Mechanism**: Flex lexer (scan.l) tokenizes; Bison parser (gram.y) builds tree\n- **Key Property**: No semantic validation; purely structural\n\n**Semantic Analyzer (analyze.c + parse_*.c)**\n- **Responsibilities**: Semantic validation, type checking, name resolution\n- **Input**: RawStmt (untyped parse tree)\n- **Output**: Query (semantically valid, type-checked)\n- **Process**:\n  - Validates table/column names against schema\n  - Type-checks expressions and coerces as needed\n  - Resolves ambiguous names\n  - Processes special clauses (aggregates, window functions, CTEs)\n- **Key Property**: Single Query per input RawStmt (usually)\n\n**Query Rewriter (rewriteHandler.c)**\n- **Responsibilities**: Rule application, view expansion, security policies\n- **Input**: Query (semantic tree)\n- **Output**: List of Queries (0..n due to expansion)\n- **Examples**:\n  - View \u2192 subquery in FROM\n  - Rule \u2192 additional auxiliary queries\n  - RLS policies \u2192 WHERE clause additions\n- **Key Property**: May expand 1 Query into many (SELECT + INSERT rule \u2192 2 queries)\n\n**Planner - Phase 1 (allpaths.c)**\n- **Responsibilities**: Generate candidate execution paths\n- **Input**: Query (semantic tree)\n- **Scope**: Base relations, joins, aggregations, etc.\n- **Process**:\n  - `generate_base_rel_paths()`: Paths for single tables\n  - `add_paths_to_joinrel()`: Join paths via nested loop, merge, hash\n  - Cost estimation via `cost_*()` functions\n- **Output**: RelOptInfo with alternative Paths (no execution specifics yet)\n\n**Planner - Phase 2 (createplan.c)**\n- **Responsibilities**: Convert optimal Path to Plan\n- **Input**: Best Path from Phase 1\n- **Process**: Recursive `create_plan()` dispatch\n  - Path type \u2192 Plan type\n  - Populates Plan node with execution parameters\n  - Example: HashPath \u2192 HashJoin with hash function, key columns\n- **Output**: Plan tree (execution-ready, immutable)\n\n**Executor - Init (execMain.c + execProcnode.c)**\n- **Responsibilities**: Build execution state tree\n- **Input**: Plan tree\n- **Process**: ExecInitNode recursive dispatch\n  - Each Plan node \u2192 PlanState node\n  - Compile expressions to ExprState\n  - Allocate runtime data structures\n  - Set up function pointers (ExecProcNode)\n- **Output**: PlanState tree with initialized state\n\n**Executor - Run (execMain.c + execProcnode.c + nodeXXX.c)**\n- **Responsibilities**: Pull tuples through pipeline\n- **Mechanism**: ExecProcNode inline dispatch\n  - Calls node function pointers\n  - Each node pulls from children\n  - Processes (filter, project, join, aggregate)\n  - Returns tuples or NULL (EOF)\n- **Key Property**: Tuple-at-a-time; streaming; no intermediate materialization (unless Sort, Agg, etc.)\n\n**Executor - End (execProcnode.c)**\n- **Responsibilities**: Clean up resources\n- **Process**: ExecEndNode recursive dispatch\n- **Handles**: Closing scans, freeing buffers, finalizing aggregates\n\n### Data Flow: Type Transformations\n\n```\nSQL String\n    \u2193\n    \u2514\u2192 raw_parser() [scan.l + gram.y]\n        \u2193\n        \u2514\u2192 RawStmt (untyped generic structure)\n            - Minimal semantic info\n            - No type checking\n            - No name resolution\n            \u2193\n            \u2514\u2192 parse_analyze() [analyze.c + parse_*.c]\n                \u2193\n                \u2514\u2192 Query (type-checked, name-resolved)\n                    - Full semantic information\n                    - Expression types resolved\n                    - Table/column references validated\n                    \u2193\n                    \u2514\u2192 QueryRewrite() [rewriteHandler.c]\n                        \u2193\n                        \u2514\u2192 Query (possibly expanded)\n                            - Rules applied\n                            - Views expanded\n                            - Security policies injected\n                            \u2193\n                            \u2514\u2192 standard_planner() [planner.c]\n                                \u251c\u2500 Phase 1: allpaths.c\n                                \u2502   \u2514\u2192 RelOptInfo (Paths with cost)\n                                \u2514\u2500 Phase 2: createplan.c\n                                    \u2193\n                                    \u2514\u2192 PlannedStmt (Plan tree + metadata)\n                                        - Executable plan\n                                        - All joins, aggregates planned\n                                        - Immutable during execution\n                                        \u2193\n                                        \u2514\u2192 ExecutorStart() [execMain.c]\n                                            \u2193\n                                            \u2514\u2192 ExecInitNode() [execProcnode.c]\n                                                \u2193\n                                                \u2514\u2192 PlanState tree\n                                                    - Runtime state\n                                                    - ExprState compiled\n                                                    - Function pointers set\n                                                    \u2193\n                                                    \u2514\u2192 ExecutorRun()\n                                                        \u2514\u2192 ExecProcNode() loop\n                                                            \u2193\n                                                            \u2514\u2192 Tuples (result set)\n```\n\n### Interface Contracts Between Components\n\n**Parser \u2192 Semantic Analyzer**\n- **Contract**: RawStmt with untyped expressions\n- **Guarantees**: Syntactically valid; may contain name/type errors\n- **Properties**: Generic structure (no knowledge of command type)\n\n**Semantic Analyzer \u2192 Rewriter**\n- **Contract**: Query with resolved types and names\n- **Guarantees**: Semantically valid within current schema; expressions typed\n- **Properties**: One-to-one mapping (usually) with input\n\n**Rewriter \u2192 Planner**\n- **Contract**: Query (may be from rewriter expansion)\n- **Guarantees**: Fully analyzed; ready for optimization\n- **Properties**: Multiple queries possible (parallel independent optimization)\n\n**Planner \u2192 Executor**\n- **Contract**: PlannedStmt with Plan tree\n- **Guarantees**: Optimal execution path selected; all joins/aggregates planned\n- **Properties**: Immutable; can be cached and reused\n\n**Executor Init \u2192 Executor Run**\n- **Contract**: PlanState tree with initialized state\n- **Guarantees**: All runtime structures allocated; expressions compiled\n- **Properties**: State tree mirrors plan structure; function pointers active\n\n### Volcano-Style Executor Dispatch (execProcnode.c)\n\nThe core dispatch mechanism at execution time is the **pull model**:\n\n**ExecProcNode inline dispatch (executor.h:310)**:\n```c\nstatic inline TupleTableSlot *\nExecProcNode(PlanState *node) {\n    if (node->chgParam != NULL)\n        ExecReScan(node);\n    return node->ExecProcNode(node);  // Function pointer\n}\n```\n\n**Dispatch during init** (`ExecInitNode`, execProcnode.c:142):\n```\nswitch (nodeTag(plan)) {\n    case T_SeqScan:\n        result = (PlanState *)ExecInitSeqScan((SeqScan *)node, ...);\n        // This sets: result->ExecProcNode = ExecSeqScan\n        break;\n    case T_NestLoop:\n        result = (PlanState *)ExecInitNestLoop((NestLoop *)node, ...);\n        // This sets: result->ExecProcNode = ExecNestLoop\n        break;\n    ...\n}\n```\n\n**Execution via function pointers** (nodeXXX.c):\n- `ExecSeqScan()` [nodeSeqscan.c]: Fetches tuples from heap sequentially\n- `ExecNestLoop()` [nodeNestloop.c]: Pulls from outer, for each outer pulls from inner, returns joined tuples\n- `ExecAgg()` [nodeAgg.c]: Accumulates tuples, returns aggregated results\n- `ExecSort()` [nodeSort.c]: Collects all input, sorts, returns in order\n- Each function:\n  1. Calls `ExecProcNode()` on children to get input tuples\n  2. Processes the tuple (filtering, projection, joining, aggregating)\n  3. Returns result tuple or NULL (EOF)\n\n**Pipeline execution in ExecutePlan()** (execMain.c:~350):\n```\nwhile (true) {\n    slot = ExecProcNode(topNode);  // Pull from root\n    if (TupIsNull(slot)) break;     // EOF\n    dest->receiveSlot(slot);         // Send to client\n}\n```\n\nThis creates a **demand-driven pipeline**:\n- Tuples flow bottom-up (pulled by parents)\n- No global queue/buffer (except for Sort, Agg, etc.)\n- Natural backpressure (if parent slow, child waits)\n- Scales to large result sets (streaming)\n\n---\n\n## Summary\n\nPostgreSQL implements a **classic multi-stage query execution pipeline** with clear separation of concerns. The **parser** (lexer + grammar) produces untyped parse trees; the **semantic analyzer** validates and types them; the **rewriter** applies rules and view expansion; the **planner** executes two-phase optimization (path generation then plan creation) to find the optimal execution strategy; and the **Volcano-style executor** pulls tuples through a tree of iterator nodes, with each node dispatched via function pointers to its type-specific implementation. This architecture achieves modularity, composability, and efficiency through clean interfaces between pipeline stages and a demand-driven execution model that streams results without materializing intermediate datasets.\n",

# PostgreSQL Query Execution Pipeline Analysis

## Files Examined

### Traffic Cop / Entry Point
- **src/backend/tcop/postgres.c** — Main traffic cop handling query protocol messages, containing `exec_simple_query()` which orchestrates the entire pipeline; also contains `pg_parse_query()`, `pg_analyze_and_rewrite_fixedparams()`, and `pg_plan_queries()` wrapper functions
- **src/backend/tcop/pquery.c** — Portal management and query execution driver; implements `CreateQueryDesc()`, `ProcessQuery()`, and `PortalRun()` which bridges planning and execution

### Parser (Lexer & Grammar)
- **src/backend/parser/parser.c** — Main entry point for parsing that calls the lexer/parser machinery
- **src/backend/parser/scan.l** — Lexer (scanner) definition in flex format; tokenizes SQL input
- **src/backend/parser/gram.y** — YACC/Bison grammar definition; parses token stream into RawStmt nodes
- **src/backend/parser/gramparse.h** — Parser header with common definitions
- **src/backend/parser/scansup.c** — Scanner support functions

### Semantic Analyzer
- **src/backend/parser/analyze.c** — Semantic analysis driver; implements `parse_analyze_fixedparams()` which transforms RawStmt into Query nodes; handles statement classification, target list processing, relation scanning, and type checking
- **src/backend/parser/parse_*.c** — Specialized analysis modules:
  - `parse_clause.c` — Processes FROM, WHERE, GROUP BY, HAVING, ORDER BY, LIMIT
  - `parse_expr.c` — Expression analysis (operators, functions, coercions)
  - `parse_agg.c` — Aggregate function analysis
  - `parse_cte.c` — Common table expression (CTE) analysis
  - `parse_relation.c` — Table and relation reference handling
  - `parse_target.c` — Target list processing
  - `parse_func.c` — Function call analysis and resolution
